---
template: blog-post
title: The Bad the Good and the Ugly
slug: /Bad_Good_Ugly
date: 2020-10-17 11:42
description: Among the objectives of this article are to learn to get around
  some limitations of old browser versions, maintain consistency between
  different browsers such as Chrome and Firefox among others as well as
  recognize that as developers we must maintain constant support to our
  applications for easy access and seamlessly experiences of our users through
  their devices of preferences regardless of their screen sides.
featuredImage: /assets/front-image.jpg
---

<!--StartFragment-->

**How to Follow Changes and Additions to CSS:**

CSS is a language that is constantly updated and / or modified. Even when you think you are a master in the use of CSS, you come across more specs or new features that you didn't know existed. The World Wide Web Consortium can help us to see current specifications and the use of different CSS features currently supported.

Here a guide to help you to find CSS support in a broad of the most popular browsers worldwide. First, we have to understand that the web developer's job is to work around to deal with different types of browsers, versions and screen sides that millions of users around the world use. Which mean that although we might not be able to develop for every single one of them, at least we should to be able to develop for the most commonly use among those browsers and screen sides.

A tip to determine if your CSS works in your specific browser is to use Queries. For example, if we convert a float item into a grid item, the float property will lose its performance and will push the items to the left, especially if we are using percentage in the float item.

![Image1](/assets/image1.jpg)

![Image2](/assets/image2.jpg "Using floats we can create a four column layout, widths and margins need to be set in %")

<!--EndFragment-->

<!--StartFragment-->

A faster way to test this type of behaviors prior to deploying is name @supports. Most of the browsers has this tool built it in for which we can use to try different properties and values and the content inside that Query will responds if it does or doesnt to support the feature. You can ask specifically if a feature is NOT supported by using @supports not.

<!--EndFragment-->

```
@supports not (display: grid) {
    .item {
        /* CSS from browsers which do not support grid layout */
    }
}

```

<!--\[if gte mso 9]><xml>
 <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
  <o:AllowPNG/>
 </o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

<!--StartFragment-->

You can also try multiple properties at the same time use and or if you need a to choose between one or another property use or

<!--EndFragment-->

```
@supports (display: grid) and (shape-outside: circle()){
    .item {
        /* CSS from browsers which support grid and CSS shapes */
    }
}

```

```
@supports (display: grid) or (display: flex){
    .item {
        /* CSS from browsers which support grid or flexbox */
    }
}

```

<!--StartFragment-->

Therefore, if you want to test a property and value for display: grid do as follows:

<!--EndFragment-->

```
.grid > .item {
    width: 23%;
    margin: 0 1%;
}

@supports(display: grid) {
    .grid {
        display: grid;
        grid-template-columns: 1fr 1fr 1fr 1fr;
        column-gap: 1%;
    }

    .grid > .item {
        width: auto;
        margin: 0;
    }
}

```

![Image 9](/assets/image9.jpg)

The complete HTML and CSS code as follows:

```
/*HTML*/
div class="grid">
  <div>One</div>
  <div>Two</div>
  <div>Three</div>
  <div>Four</div>
</div>



/* CSS*/
* { box-sizing: border-box; }

body {
  margin: 40px;
  background-color: #fff;
  color: #444;
  font: 1.4em Arial, sans-serif;
}

.grid {
  display: grid;
  grid-template-columns: 1fr 1fr 1fr 1fr;
  grid-auto-rows: 100px;
  grid-gap: 20px;
}

.grid > * {
padding: 10px;
  border: 5px solid rgba(214,129,137,.5);
  border-radius: 5px;
  background-color: rgba(233,78,119,.5);
  color: #fff;
  float: left;  
  width: 23%;
  margin: 0 1%;
}

@supports (display:grid) {
  .grid > * {
    width: auto;
    margin: 0;
  }
}

```

  <o:AllowPNG/>
 </o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

<!--StartFragment-->

All the previous does not really need to be inside a feature query. In this example, the things that must be inside the query are the margin and width properties. These properties are needed for old browsers.

<!--EndFragment-->

<!--\[if gte mso 9]><xml>
 <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
  <o:AllowPNG/>
 </o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

  <w:LidThemeComplexScript>X-NONE</w:LidThemeComplexScript>

   <w:BreakWrappedTables/>

<!--StartFragment-->

Although it is extremely difficult to have exactly the same design of a website in all browsers, there are always ways to maintain consistency between them in such a way that we can reach a wider target audience.

<!--EndFragment-->

<!--StartFragment-->

Although the developer tends to develop towards how works better mainly in Google chrome, due to its position as the leader browsers in the market share, we should not under any circumstances overlook support for any other browser.

![Market share](/assets/image11.jpg "Market Share")



A good way to figure out which CSS properties are supported from different browsers is by checking it on <https://caniuse.com/>

Graceful degradations Vs Progressive Enhancement are responsive strategies that a developer can take when developing for different browsers and its different versions.

**Graceful degradation** is when a website is built a design with the latest features modern browsers can handled, and from there gradually coding downwards to provide support for older browsers.

**Progressive enhancement** is when a website is built, at first, with a basic baseline version of the website, and gradually it builds up improve versions to support advanced functionality

<!--EndFragment-->

![Graceful Degradation Vs Progressive Enhancement](/assets/image12.jpg "Resposive Strategies")
